Importing Excel Data in R - A Quick Update

2018/01/17

Back in 2015 I wrote a blog post on importing Excel tables into R. Happily for everyone this is now a lot easier than it was. This post provides a quick update on importing and exporting to Excel.

Import Directly from the RStudio Menu

The big change is that it is now very easy to import using the RStudio Menu: File > Import Dataset > From Excel.

Next, navigate to the file that you want to import and select it. You will then see something like this.

One point to bear in mind is that the import will often default to the name dataset so that you need to make sure you enter a meaningful name for the dataset.

If your workbook has multiple sheets then you can choose a sheet number or choose to skip rows if you have a bunch of filler junk in the top rows.

You can also click on a column and choose to skip it or change the format.

It is also worth bearing in mind that if you are importing a number of worksheets you can lose track. I sometimes use the approach of copying the import chunk into an Rmarkdown document to keep track of what I am doing and where a file came from.

library(tidyverse)
library(readxl)
taxonomy <- read_excel("/Users/pauloldham17inch/Desktop/taxonomy_final.xlsx")
taxonomy

As we can see from importing the file behind the scenes RStudio is using the readxl library to import the file.

We can also see that there are some messages that are generated during the import process. For example this dataset generated a long string of warnings that looked like this.

Expecting logical in AH5501 / R5501C34: got 'Aaptos suberitoides'Expecting logical in AH5502 / R5502C34: got 'Abdopus abaculus'Expecting logical in AH5503 / R5503C34: got 'Abdopus aculeatus'Expecting logical in AH5504 / R5504C34: got 'Abralia armata'Expecting logical in AH5505 / R5505C34: got 'Abraliopsis hoylei'Expecting logical in AH5506 / R5506C34: got 'Abudefduf bengalensis'Expecting logical in AH5507 / R5507C34: got 'Abudefduf sexfasciatus'

These arise because readxl guesses the column type by reading the top 1000 rows for each column. However, where a column contains a mix of numbers or characters this can lead to an expecting integer/expecting logical type of error. A lot of the time this is not actually a problem. However, it is important to pay attention to the warnings because it may in fact be a problem indicating some kind of corruption in your data.

To fix this you can try a number of options

1. Use the guess_max argument

Use the guess_max argument to increase the number of rows that are read to guess the column type. In the case of our example dataset this didn’t work because the problems are found in the last few rows but it often will. You can add an n_max value (shown below as NULL) where you know the maximum number of rows.

library(readxl)
taxonomy <- read_excel("/Users/pauloldham17inch/Desktop/open_source_master/asean/data-taxonomy/taxonomy_final.xlsx", guess_max = min(2000, n_max = NULL))

An alternative to this approach is simply to set min as the maximum number of rows.

library(readxl)
taxonomy <- read_excel("/Users/pauloldham17inch/Desktop/taxonomy_final.xlsx", guess_max = min(8400, n_max = NULL))

2. Specify the column types

If that doesn’t work for you then the third option is to work out what the format should be and pass it as a string.

A toy example is the following data frame that we can write to excel (below).

df <- tibble(a = c(1,2,3), b = c("a", "b", "c"), d = c(TRUE, FALSE, TRUE), e = c("2017-12-10", "20170815", "2017_06_12")) %>% writexl::write_xlsx(., "df.xlsx")

When we read this in we specify the column types. Note that in this case we need to use the term “text” rather than the familiar “character” or we get an error.

df <- read_excel("/Users/pauloldham17inch/Desktop/open_source_master/blog/content/post/df.xlsx", col_names = TRUE, col_types = c("numeric", "text", "logical", "text"))

The documentation for read_excel sets out quite a few other options. For example we could specify the format of some columns and leave the function to guess the others. That would look like this.

df <- read_excel("/Users/pauloldham17inch/Desktop/open_source_master/blog/content/post/df.xlsx", col_names = TRUE, col_types = c("guess", "guess", "logical", "guess"))

2. Convert all columns to a single type

For a dataset with a lot of columns trying to work out the column types or writing guess, logical, character can rapidly become painful. Depending on your needs it is often easier to simply use the col_types = "text" for all columns and change them where needed later using as.character(), as.logical(), as.numeric() or as.Date().

library(readxl)
taxonomy <- read_excel("/Users/pauloldham17inch/Desktop/taxonomy_final.xlsx", col_types = "text")
taxonomy
## # A tibble: 8,400 x 50
##            scientificname       type genusorabove specificepithet parsed
##                     <chr>      <chr>        <chr>           <chr>  <chr>
##  1          abramis brama SCIENTIFIC      Abramis           brama   TRUE
##  2 acanthamoeba polyphaga SCIENTIFIC Acanthamoeba       polyphaga   TRUE
##  3  acaudina molpadioides SCIENTIFIC     Acaudina    molpadioides   TRUE

## #   terrestrial <chr>, noenvironment <chr>, worms_id <chr>

With our toy dataset we could easily change the columns that are our target as needed.

df$a <- as.numeric(df$a)

Dates can be troublesome and in cases where you need to format date fields the lubridate package will really make your life a whole lot easier.

In our toy dataset case while the dates are all in YYYYMMDD format (and those in your dataset may not be) the separators are different and using as.Date() won’t work for the second and third dates.

as.Date(df$e)
## [1] "2017-12-10" NA           NA

However, this problem is easily handled by lubridate::as_date

library(lubridate)
df$e <- as_date(df$e)

Charlotte Wickham offers an incredibly useful DataCamp course Working with Dates and Times in R that will have you up and running in no time.

Tidying column names

One issue once you have your data in R is that column names in excel files often contain mixed case names and spaces or other characters such as brackets that can be awkward to work with in R. To solve that an easy option is to use the recent janitor package.

install.packages("janitor")
library(janitor)
df <- janitor::clean_names(df)

This does a pretty good job of cleaning up names but is not perfect. If you have particular needs try using the stringr package that is now part of the tidyverse packages. Assuming you have used install:packages("tidyverse") you can then call the library and use the following stringr function to tidy the names up.

library(tidyverse)
names(df) <- str_replace_all(names(df),"[(]|[)]|[[:space:]]", "_")

I’ve chosen some of the harder ones to remember here as brackets need to be escaped. The above will replace brackets and spaces with an underscore. Whether that is what you want is another matter but it will help you to get started. If you need more help try the Basic Regular Expressions Cheatsheet in R or the chapter on strings in Hadley Wickham’s book R for Data Science. Charlotte Wickham also offers a Data Camp course on String Manipulation in R with stringr. When it comes to working with strings stringr is your friend and if that doesn’t solve the problem then try the stringi package.

Exporting to Excel

In the earlier post I wrote about using write.xlsx() from the xlsx package. However, I have now switched over to the writexl package as I find it easier to remember and use. It is also an ROpenSci package and I use a lot of ROpenSci packages. writexl has the added bonus that Clippy appears in the documentation to brighten up your day… or drive you insane.

install.packages("writexl")

We simply specify the file and the name of the file we want to write. An additional argument col_names = TRUE is set to TRUE by default so you only need to specify that if you want the value to be FALSE.

library(writexl)
writexl::write_xlsx(df, path = "df.xlsx", col_names = TRUE)

You can also write multiple sheets by specifying the data frames in a list and passing them to write_xlsx.

#TO OD
#tmp <- list("df1", "df2")
#write_xlsx(tmp, "tmp.xlsx")

There we have it, reading and writing Excel files in R if you have to is now really very easy.

# cant read in directly from google drive. How annoying
#test <- readxl::read_xlsx("https://drive.google.com/open?id=0B4piiKOCkRPDNWhrdGxXc0YwTk0&authuser=0")
readxl::read_excel("https://github.com/wipo-analytics/opensource-patent-analytics/opensource-patent-analytics/2_datasets/pizza_all_24294/patentscope_pizza_1940_2005_9659.xls")

https://stackoverflow.com/questions/14441729/read-a-csv-from-github-into-r

download.file("https://github.com/wipo-analytics/opensource-patent-analytics/blob/master/2_datasets/pizza_all_24294/patentscope_pizza_1940_2005_9659.xls?raw=true", destfile = "/tmp/test.xls", method = "curl")

From SO: read_excel(tf) https://stackoverflow.com/questions/41368628/read-excel-file-from-a-url-using-the-readxl-package

Answer by Luke A

library(tidyverse)
library(readxl)
library(httr)
# should recognise if the file ext is .xls or .xlsx

readxl_online <- function(url, type = NULL, ...){
  #test <- stringr::str_detect(url, "[.]xlsx")
  #if (test == TRUE) {
  #  type = 
    
  #}
  
  df <- httr::GET(url, write_disk()) %>%
    tempfile(fileext = type) %>% 
    read_excel()
}
readxl_online("https://github.com/wipo-analytics/opensource-patent-analytics/blob/master/2_datasets/pizza_all_24294/patentscope_pizza_1940_2005_9659.xls?raw=true", type = ".xls")
GET("https://github.com/wipo-analytics/opensource-patent-analytics/blob/master/2_datasets/pizza_all_24294/patentscope_pizza_1940_2005_9659.xls?raw=true", write_disk(tf <- tempfile(fileext = ".xls")))

Round Up

Reading excel data into R and writing to excel is now a lot easier than it was a couple of years ago thanks to the work of Hadley Wickham and Jenny Bryan in writing readxl. writexl by

If you need would like to learn more on importing data into R then try the DataCamp course on Importing Data & Cleaning with R that covers Excel.
